### content/nazir/39.txt
<a id="src-00147"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nazir/39.txt`

Nazir 39 – The direction of hair growth
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEg3fAGFXTOgfGmVgLZZh8nrSrwGwUJ0GfO-ggrqXYC5PNlGWbKLhDCp5ouRFmNujikZztfyAlKtQSBqvj2kwa3dhu6cMDcl6d_WiBCpo9vruxyAYgWsbJ3rm-hnsZTv5_k1PxYrk61kVK2l/s1600/Portrait+of+a+bearded+old+man+by+Rembrandt+Van+Rijn.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="320" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEg3fAGFXTOgfGmVgLZZh8nrSrwGwUJ0GfO-ggrqXYC5PNlGWbKLhDCp5ouRFmNujikZztfyAlKtQSBqvj2kwa3dhu6cMDcl6d_WiBCpo9vruxyAYgWsbJ3rm-hnsZTv5_k1PxYrk61kVK2l/s320/Portrait+of+a+bearded+old+man+by+Rembrandt+Van+Rijn.jpg" width="244" /></a>If a nazir shaves his head – or bandits grab him and shave it – he must wait at least thirty more days before concluding his term. This is because the standard (and the minimal) term is thirty days, and when he shaves his hair, it must have at least thirty days of growth.
<br />
<br />
A question: is the hair growing from the root or the end? Why would we want to know? – If the hair is growing from its root, and the bandits gave him a haircut but left seven days' worth of hair growth on his head – does he now wait for thirty days still, or only for twenty-three? For example, if the hair grows from the root, then that hair he consecrated has grown down by now and was cut by the bandits, so he will have to wait another thirty days. But if the hair grows from the end down, then he will only need twenty-three days. So?
<br />
<br />
Let's look at a nit (louse). It always remains at the root – so it must be that the hair grows from the end! – Not so fast! Maybe the nit is alive and keeps crawling to the root. Well, let's look at a dead nit – it is always found at the end of the hair, so it must grow from the root! – No, because perhaps when the nit is dead and cannot resist gravity, it keeps sliding down as the hair grows. 
<br />
<br />
The final proof comes from the beards of old men who die them black. We see that the whitish color of gray hair appears at the root – so it is proven that it grows from the root!<br />
<br />
Art: Portrait of a bearded old man by Rembrandt Van Rijn

---

### content/nazir/4.txt
<a id="src-00148"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nazir/4.txt`

Nazir 4 – To be like Samson
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;">
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEhtRTSkjQyCnoJAjodCW3HQ2kjluKct-S32jKNFt9n9errAYGMAF6JVfhBZrSHSkmKOpcp31vLnDJLjrRpMKPbs0NfN_CU11sZM-OfICidJHU3t48JGv2RAqhnIBdL4TML583mJCUXgeVDG/s1600/The+Wedding+of+Samson+Rembrandt+Van+Rijn.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="231" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEhtRTSkjQyCnoJAjodCW3HQ2kjluKct-S32jKNFt9n9errAYGMAF6JVfhBZrSHSkmKOpcp31vLnDJLjrRpMKPbs0NfN_CU11sZM-OfICidJHU3t48JGv2RAqhnIBdL4TML583mJCUXgeVDG/s320/The+Wedding+of+Samson+Rembrandt+Van+Rijn.jpg" width="320" /></a></div>
If one accepts to be a nazir and to abstain only from grapes – he is a complete nazir, with all the prohibitions. Since the Torah forbade separately "...from new wine and aged wine," we see that partial prohibition has the force to impose a complete nazir vow on him. 
<br />
<br />
The above is the view of the Sages. Rabbi Shimon disagrees: since the Torah said, "From anything made of grapevine," we see that only a complete declaration takes effect. Each disputant, the Sages and Rabbi Shimon, then explains away the other one's proof.
<br />
<br />
One can become a "nazir like Samson" or even just a "permanent nazir." The permanent nazir observes the laws of wine, ritual impurity, and not cutting hair all his life. However, if his hair is too heavy, he can trim it – and bring sacrifices – then continue. "Nazir like Samson" cannot cut his hair at all, but on the other hand, he is not bringing sacrifice even if he becomes impure – he just purifies himself and continues. Others say that "Nazir like Samson" does not exist – because Samson never became a nazir himself. 
<br />
<br />
The categories of "Nazir like Samsom" and "permanent nazir" are nowhere mentioned in the Torah but constitute part of "unwritten laws." These were initially taught only from teacher to student and later recorded.
<br />
<br />
Art: The Wedding of Samson Rembrandt Van Rijn

---

### content/nazir/40.txt
<a id="src-00149"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nazir/40.txt`

Nazir 40 – Real shaving is done with a razor
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEip7d8pcKO0FnwM1ilbY2A-gE0oTYV6VbMV9FclV-cVB6__hZZAM7u_UxYybtf7qYw6Fw7KSbUhWvG_fFZaW_FDjA-XBXytaXZNbdiwPUAucyG0KjGEV6Zy9TN_DeDauVRw2HWN46RLIH5R/s1600/Portrait+of+a+Clean-shaven+Man+by+Lucas+The+Elder+Cranach.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="320" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEip7d8pcKO0FnwM1ilbY2A-gE0oTYV6VbMV9FclV-cVB6__hZZAM7u_UxYybtf7qYw6Fw7KSbUhWvG_fFZaW_FDjA-XBXytaXZNbdiwPUAucyG0KjGEV6Zy9TN_DeDauVRw2HWN46RLIH5R/s320/Portrait+of+a+Clean-shaven+Man+by+Lucas+The+Elder+Cranach.jpg" width="220" /></a>The Torah said about a nazir that a "<a href="http://bible.ort.org/books/pentd2.asp?ACTION=displaypage&amp;BOOK=4&amp;CHAPTER=6#P3826">razor shall not pass over his head</a>." However, the words "razor" or really "no razor" is already sufficient for us to know the law. Then why are the words "will not pass over his head" written at all? – to include any other way of hair removal, such as pincers or depilatory, and to make it prohibited for nazir to remove his hair in any way. 
<br />
<br />
But now the word "razor" becomes extra: if, in the end, the Torah is going to prohibit all kinds of hair removal, then why mention "razor?" Now, this teaches an additional law: that when the nazir concludes his term and shaves his head, that shaving must be done only with a razor. If he uses any other instrument, the shaving does not count; he has to re-do the last thirty days of a nazir and shave again, this time correctly. 
<br />
<br />
There are, in general, three categories of people who must shave all their hair: a nazir, a <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tzaraath">metzora</a> (spiritual leper), and all Levites on the day of their first inauguration  for Temple service. The Talmud tries to derive the laws of one of these categories from the others.  
<br />
<br />
Art: Portrait of a Clean-shaven Man by Lucas The Elder Cranach

### content/nazir/43.txt
<a id="src-00152"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nazir/43.txt`

Nazir 43 - How to get multiple punishments
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEgb_5e5AUOc8sUl0wthwegPUC4YZpPTcbpbjf7La6c_f0wXBkbhfhb71mRUPiir0vO4QK_isQ7fKFWu0zF8GQCyCAxhboX8oT0Ui7501eZg7rC5SuDjGc1OpN33sEuV_8x8LhwMZo6PAyEC/s1600/Peasants+Drinking+In+A+Tavern+by+Jan+Miense++Molenaer.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="320" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEgb_5e5AUOc8sUl0wthwegPUC4YZpPTcbpbjf7La6c_f0wXBkbhfhb71mRUPiir0vO4QK_isQ7fKFWu0zF8GQCyCAxhboX8oT0Ui7501eZg7rC5SuDjGc1OpN33sEuV_8x8LhwMZo6PAyEC/s320/Peasants+Drinking+In+A+Tavern+by+Jan+Miense++Molenaer.jpg" width="242" /></a>If a nazir is warned not to drink wine, and then he drinks all day, he is liable only for the first drink. However, if they warn him again before every sip, he is liable (to lashes) for each separate act.<br />
<br />
Similarly, if they tell him not to become ritually impure by coming in contact with a dead body, and he does it, he is liable only once. Still, he is liable multiple times if they keep warning him before each act.<br />
<br />
But how can one be liable for ritual impurity more than once? Once he is impure - that's it, and he cannot become any more impure than this!? - He can achieve it by coming into a house where there is a dead body (for this is a separate prohibition) and then actually becoming impure.<br />
<br />
However, this is not possible. If his nose comes in first, he is already impure but has not entirely come into the house. And if he leans back, so his nose is in line with his body, his toe comes in first!<br />
<br />
We can invent a solution: he should come in while in a big box, since big boxes protect from impurity. And then somebody must remove the roof of his box. For this, he really does get punished twice - provided that he assists in removing the roof, for otherwise, it is not he but the one who removed the roof would be to blame. Thus, we found an answer.<br />
<br />
Art:&nbsp;Peasants Drinking In A Tavern by Jan Miense &nbsp;Molenaer

---

### content/nazir/47.txt
<a id="src-00156"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nazir/47.txt`

Nazir 47 – Who is more important, nazir or High Priest?
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEi1uDGHix9BLnK6bVddUjfVMGVSqnYmC64wRxP0N3-dG7cfRyX277Qw7Ckefb-JlHU2bMjocQM8GA5ql4xwAE_p92njTyqxK84z_f5PNlzgdCSA8Bs6Oiq5H_T9QwbTn0xoKe2k4aBUKxGE/s1600/Burying+the+Dead+by+Michael+Sweerts.JPG" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="221" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEi1uDGHix9BLnK6bVddUjfVMGVSqnYmC64wRxP0N3-dG7cfRyX277Qw7Ckefb-JlHU2bMjocQM8GA5ql4xwAE_p92njTyqxK84z_f5PNlzgdCSA8Bs6Oiq5H_T9QwbTn0xoKe2k4aBUKxGE/s320/Burying+the+Dead+by+Michael+Sweerts.JPG" width="320" /></a>The High Priest and nazir have this in common: they are not allowed to become ritually impure by touching a dead body. And yet, if any of them finds an unattended corpse so that the obligation to bury the body devolves on him, he should attend to it, even though he will become ritually impure.
<br />
<br />
What if a High Priest and a nazir find this corpse together – who is obligated to bury this dead and thus preserve the other one from becoming impure? This should be the High Priest because to purify himself, he only needs to wait for seven days and get himself sprinkled with the ashes of the Red Heifer. By contrast, a nazir would need to bring special sacrifices before he can restart being a nazir. This is the opinion of Rabbi Eliezer. The Sages say just the opposite. Nazir should bury because his limitations are temporary, whereas the High Priest should always be pure.
<br />
<br />
In this argument, the positions are clearly stated. But now the Talmud compares other gradations, such as the Priest Anointed for War and a Deputy High Priest; who is more important? This is resolved as follows: to preserve the life of the Priest Anointed for War, he is more critical because he serves the needs of many people. But concerning purity, the Deputy is more important concerning purity because he can be called to serve in the Temple any time the chief High Priest becomes impure.
<br />
<br />
Art: Burying the Dead by Michael Sweerts

---

### content/nazir/52.txt
<a id="src-00162"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nazir/52.txt`

Nazir 52 – Skull and spine
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;">
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEiWYtiOa9or4Op_YkYjLjpFF8ZI4qSNv6Rpu_nBSL9Ij488kIviGNoCZ8XWMNAM4ky5zL34hDapFgQF9Ewc3y3kBGp0smjqAuxDKaDTe3Z5zzAdEuj4jEefVJoEKWHYMgif9QEBFeuiaYNU/s1600/An+old+man%252C+holding+a+skull+by+Jan+Lievens.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="320" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEiWYtiOa9or4Op_YkYjLjpFF8ZI4qSNv6Rpu_nBSL9Ij488kIviGNoCZ8XWMNAM4ky5zL34hDapFgQF9Ewc3y3kBGp0smjqAuxDKaDTe3Z5zzAdEuj4jEefVJoEKWHYMgif9QEBFeuiaYNU/s320/An+old+man%252C+holding+a+skull+by+Jan+Lievens.jpg" width="249" /></a></div>
If a nazir comes in touch with a dead body, he loses his previous days, has to shave, must bring sacrifices, and then restart. How much of a dead body? One example is a skull and spine – just bones, even if they have no meat.
<br />
<br />This rule had two versions; one read "skull and spine," but the other read "skull or spine." According to the second one, a skull or a spine is enough for a nazir to lose his days and shave. Which one is right?
<br />
<br />
The Talmud tries to resolve this in many ways. For example, since there are six questions related to nazir where Rabbi Akiva initially held a more stringent position but eventually conceded to the Sages, if his position was more stringent here, too, and read "skull or spine," - then the total count would be seven, not six. However, Rabbi Shimon says there was another rule about a "quarter-measurement of blood from two different deads," which Rabbi Akiva never conceded. This changes the count and ruins our proof.
<br />
<br />
Incidentally, Rabbi Shimon emphasized his points: "While Rabbi Akiva was alive, he never changed his mind on this question, and whether he retracted after death - I don't know about that." Unfortunately, there were more refined ways of putting it. Rabbi Shimon felt remorse and fasted to atone for it until his teeth darkened.
<br />
<br />
Art: An old man, holding a skull by Jan Lievens

---

### content/nazir/54.txt
<a id="src-00164"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nazir/54.txt`

Nazir 54 – Not to shave!
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;">
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEgaiwbcbMN2iZflGDq8U6Yr6nvGnec2L-yeWMjMSlBO6zxljO3gQP_fLEN8yi3VioK-JT_HH6s9J8RqhucT4sRmU1y1op0jIKPq3FHhWZ5SX8e9pGlF7xmR2_-kTdz0YsgrSlQL0cMGnuVJ/s1600/Plowing+ox+By+Rudolf+Koller.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="209" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEgaiwbcbMN2iZflGDq8U6Yr6nvGnec2L-yeWMjMSlBO6zxljO3gQP_fLEN8yi3VioK-JT_HH6s9J8RqhucT4sRmU1y1op0jIKPq3FHhWZ5SX8e9pGlF7xmR2_-kTdz0YsgrSlQL0cMGnuVJ/s320/Plowing+ox+By+Rudolf+Koller.jpg" width="320" /></a></div>
There are cases when the ritual impurity does not really exist, but it was “decreed by the Sages.” Take, for example, a field where a grave was previously located, then the location of it was lost, and then the field was plowed over. Since anyone who is walking through the field can potentially touch a bone and become impure, the Sages said that such a field should be treated as ritually impure, and anyone who went through it without precautions will have to purify himself.
<br />
<br />
However, from the Torah point of view, accidentally touching a bone is unlikely because a plow does not usually reach that deep, and the person is really pure. A nazir who becomes impure should cut his hair and bring sacrifices, but a nazir who is pure is not allowed to touch even one hair. What is a nazir to do if he passes through a “field full of fragments," as it is called?
<br />
<br />
Here is what he should do: purify himself with the ashes of the red heifer on days three and seven, and then continue being a nazir. The seven days of his impurity are removed from his count, but otherwise, he continues to count as before. And he does not shave his hair or bring sacrifices because, in truth, he is pure. Other similar cases, such as being under the same branch as a corpse (but not a real roof that transmits impurity) are treated the same way.
<br />
<br />
If, in addition to being a nazir, he became a metzora (spiritual leper), he purifies himself from that but continues being a nazir since metzora, even though it requires purification, is not the same as the impurity of the dead. Thus, he does not lose all the previous days, as he would if we were to go to a cemetery.
<br />
<br />
Art: Plowing ox By Rudolf Koller

---

### content/nazir/55.txt
<a id="src-00165"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nazir/55.txt`

Nazir 55 – Nazir outside of Israel?
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEhS_ADtOjtGJFTOvlDTvLg46yFBPyzrMFyxLSG3abUI-PkKfm7T-xBpAMldH6BjvxxJ3dSiubhyphenhyphenyDeHe-64zR8_gsM3ljPNm7YPpCAXgz2dY663EzY2aWORV3JPgQ-iKAuQqX9RLO5Eqj-5/s1600/Graveyard+under+Snow+by+Caspar+David+Friedrich.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="320" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEhS_ADtOjtGJFTOvlDTvLg46yFBPyzrMFyxLSG3abUI-PkKfm7T-xBpAMldH6BjvxxJ3dSiubhyphenhyphenyDeHe-64zR8_gsM3ljPNm7YPpCAXgz2dY663EzY2aWORV3JPgQ-iKAuQqX9RLO5Eqj-5/s320/Graveyard+under+Snow+by+Caspar+David+Friedrich.jpg" width="256" /></a>The Sages decreed that anyone who left Israel would immediately become ritually impure to discourage people from leaving the Land of Israel. Ostensibly, this is because people who live outside of Israel are not as careful marking off graves (ground-based impurity), and one can pass over an unknown grave. However, the Sages may have declared the airspace outside Israel impure (air-based impurity).
<br />
<br />
The laws of nazir may shed some light on this. A nazir who went outside of Israel becomes ritually impure and will have to continue his days after he purifies himself. We also saw that he purifies himself with the ashes of Red Heifer. Now, the ashes are only needed for the impurity of the dead. This proves that the out-of-Israel impurity was because of the unmarked graves, and it is a ground-based impurity, does it not? – Actually, no. It could be that this is sheer air-based impurity, and the ashes of the Red Heifer are mentioned only regarding a <a href="http://mkerzner.blogspot.com/2015/10/nazir-54-not-to-shave.html">plowed-over cemetery</a>&nbsp;but not anything else.
<br />
<br />
Art: Graveyard under Snow by Caspar David Friedrich

---

### content/nazir/56.txt
<a id="src-00166"></a>

### content/nazir/57.txt
<a id="src-00167"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nazir/57.txt`

Nazir 57 – One out of two nazirs
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEjAo69eFnFgltKZt7YE7p-OS4e9vfCp2HjG8ZqX9sqpZ4skPlfNU27aBGm6aQUZ6yrPpiSkuegK9dRutmZHuzUe6kB12AvosytrIMrvJNrKx9psJVEYnx6XJGvAsp3EP1swHXcxgEJxw1dL/s1600/Portrait+of+a+Clean-Shaven+Young+Man+by+Lucas+The+Elder+Cranach.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="320" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEjAo69eFnFgltKZt7YE7p-OS4e9vfCp2HjG8ZqX9sqpZ4skPlfNU27aBGm6aQUZ6yrPpiSkuegK9dRutmZHuzUe6kB12AvosytrIMrvJNrKx9psJVEYnx6XJGvAsp3EP1swHXcxgEJxw1dL/s320/Portrait+of+a+Clean-Shaven+Young+Man+by+Lucas+The+Elder+Cranach.jpg" width="227" /></a>If two nazirs (correctly, nazirim) were walking together on the road, and somebody saw that one of them became ritually impure, they have a problem on their hands. The impure one must shave and bring sacrifices to purify himself, and the pure one simply continues being a nazir. But they don't know who is who.<br />
<br />
Here is their way out. They both continue being nazir until the end of their term. Then they both shave because it would be required for both pure and impure nazir. Then they take two sets of sacrifices, one for a nazir who is pure, and another, a different set, for one who is impure, and bring them together to the Temple. 
<br />
<br />
Each of them now says: "If I am really pure, and my friend is impure, then the sacrifices of purity are mine, and the other set is his." Then they bring both sets. But this works only for a pure nazir. Now they continue for yet another thirty days, and at the end, they bring just one set of sacrifices – for the nazir who is pure. Each one says: "If I was the pure one – then this sacrifice is my friend's, and previously my sacrifice was already brought. But if I was the impure one, then this sacrifice is now mine."<br />
<br />
The Talmud then asks: why is there any doubt at all? We know how to resolve all such doubts based on the laws of the doubts of a suspected wife (next Tractate). That is, if the suspected people hid, the doubt is confirmed. And if they were in a public place, there is no doubt at all. Here they did not hide from the one who saw them!? – He was far, so it is as if they were hiding.
<br />
<br />
Art: Portrait of a Clean-Shaven Young Man by Lucas The Elder Cranach

---

### content/nazir/58.txt
<a id="src-00168"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nazir/58.txt`

### content/nazir/59.txt
<a id="src-00169"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nazir/59.txt`

Nazir 59 – Two nazirs, complicated further
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;">
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEg0-oIc81LRLgjyygSUE1JbUijRk3O4fMSSw6iuXlhEs2qPYOjW_F3QCtxNFVKceV3Lx3ojQG1Vvy9CL5mnK644Di9M9flZZRgg9I-64V6JVPfkR0mcPhyphenhyphenz-P5tMxAoUx446CpGU4i_wqO4/s1600/David+Street+in+Jerusalem+by+Gustave+Bauernfeind.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="320" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEg0-oIc81LRLgjyygSUE1JbUijRk3O4fMSSw6iuXlhEs2qPYOjW_F3QCtxNFVKceV3Lx3ojQG1Vvy9CL5mnK644Di9M9flZZRgg9I-64V6JVPfkR0mcPhyphenhyphenz-P5tMxAoUx446CpGU4i_wqO4/s320/David+Street+in+Jerusalem+by+Gustave+Bauernfeind.jpg" width="244" /></a></div>
Continuing the <a href="http://mkerzner.blogspot.com/2015/10/nazir-57-one-out-of-two-nazirs.html">story of two nazirs</a>, one who became ritually impure and had to bring sacrifices together, making conditional statements about whose sacrifice it was, let us suppose that one of them dies. Now the surviving one does not have a friend with whom to make those conditions. What is he to do?
<br />
<br />
He must find someone "in the street" willing to help. This volunteer has to put himself in a condition of doubt by saying,  "I adopt to be a nazir, but conditionally. If the survivor was impure, I am a nazir immediately." After thirty days, they bring the same two sets of sacrifices, one required for a pure nazir and one for an impure one, and declare that if the survivor was impure, then the impure sacrifices are his, and the other ones are for the volunteer. But if the survivor was pure, the pure sacrifices are his, and the impure ones are offered as sacrifices in doubt (since such a category exists). Then the volunteer continues, "If the surviving nazir was pure, then my term of being a nazir starts after thirty days." Then they keep offering more sacrifices and making more conditions.
<br />
<br />
This was the opinion of Rabbi Yehoshua. But Ben  Zome asked, "Who will go out to this extent to extricate his friend out of his doubts?" Rather, the survivor should bring the minimal sacrifice that would allow him to conclude his being a nazir, even though they involve bird sacrifices, which is not standard - but it will enable him to achieve his purpose. Even though Rabbi Yehoshua argued that this was not the preferred way, the Sages agreed to Ben Zoma as more practical.
<br />
<br />
A question to Rabbi Yehoshua: "What is really wrong with Ben Zoma's solution?" - Actually, nothing; Rabbi Yehoshua just wanted to sharpen the minds of his students with a more complicated one.<br />
<br />
Art: David Street in Jerusalem by Gustave Bauernfeind

### content/nazir/61.txt
<a id="src-00172"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nazir/61.txt`

Nazir 61 – Who can become a nazir?
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;">
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEjBXJawfiWY5R_WQgSQpfPGXvlxIz3i-A-nXrNNzWc8kt-iBADKSSZAkbNO5oaOgiHN996ZOufdeNaNIKWi8BODG_0HriCDOfOahKHWzI9FiJ5fnh1pF74eyhfL3z66ZAD8TVz8IUPSyn3b/s1600/Burial+on+The+Plains+by+Richard+Lorenz.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="224" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEjBXJawfiWY5R_WQgSQpfPGXvlxIz3i-A-nXrNNzWc8kt-iBADKSSZAkbNO5oaOgiHN996ZOufdeNaNIKWi8BODG_0HriCDOfOahKHWzI9FiJ5fnh1pF74eyhfL3z66ZAD8TVz8IUPSyn3b/s320/Burial+on+The+Plains+by+Richard+Lorenz.jpg" width="320" /></a></div>
A Jewish man can become a nazir, but a non-Jew cannot. Also, a Jewish woman can become a nazir (nazirah), and one can even force his slave to become a nazir.
<br />
<br />
All three rules above are not obvious. Why is it that a non-Jew cannot become a nazir? – Because the Torah said, "<a href="http://bible.ort.org/books/pentd2.asp?ACTION=displaypage&amp;BOOK=4&amp;CHAPTER=6#P3823">Speak to the sons of Israel</a>," but not to other peoples. However, this cannot serve as proof because later, the Torah uses the term "man," which should mean "any man." In fact, a non-Jew can bring sacrifices in the Temple, so why can't  he be a nazir?
<br />
<br />
Perhaps it is because a nazir is not allowed to bury even his father, and maybe for a non-Jew, it is different? – Can't be because inheritance laws apply to all people, including inheriting from the father. Or perhaps it is because a nazir should avoid impurity, and a non-Jew, since he does not go to the Temple and does not have the concept of impurity, cannot be a nazir? – No, that is not convincing either: perhaps he does have a concept of impurity, just that he gets no punishment for being impure!?
<br />
<br />
The Talmud tries a few more attempts but concludes that this rule is impossible to prove. Rabbi Yochanan summarized that God tells this law directly to Moses and not written anywhere in the Torah.
<br />
<br />
Art: Burial on The Plains by Richard Lorenz

---

### content/nazir/62.txt
<a id="src-00173"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nazir/62.txt`

Nazir 62 – Willing slaves
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;">
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEjC6iL_ZZTqij-QQH4XdurRf5caFjFScXxoQpJPxJcrOGTIqsRneoA3TJUubIyv34jdfRE2a515DfHWUPLfrirE-f2zeCLc4dP8ixhuvt7N5MrtyLbOuxAgIw5H-SZEJ3ol3Bijdj88D8kT/s1600/Slaves+on+the+West+Coast+of+Africa+by+Francois-Auguste+Biard.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="229" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEjC6iL_ZZTqij-QQH4XdurRf5caFjFScXxoQpJPxJcrOGTIqsRneoA3TJUubIyv34jdfRE2a515DfHWUPLfrirE-f2zeCLc4dP8ixhuvt7N5MrtyLbOuxAgIw5H-SZEJ3ol3Bijdj88D8kT/s320/Slaves+on+the+West+Coast+of+Africa+by+Francois-Auguste+Biard.jpg" width="320" /></a></div>
A slave acquired by a Jew has to undergo circumcision and accept on himself the majority of the mitzvot: he will be obligated to keep all the "don't do” prohibitions but not all of the “do” commandments. Thus, his obligations are the same as a Jewish woman keeps.
<br />
<br />
What happens if a slave does not want to undergo circumcision or accept the commandments? – The master can keep him for a year to see if he changes his mind, but afterward, must sell him back.
<br />
<br />
Another uncommon feature of such slaves is that if the master accidentally or intentionally kicks out his tooth, eye, or finger, the slave goes to freedom. Once any slave acquires his freedom – he cannot be made a slave again. In fact, he becomes a full Jew, with the additional mitzvot of a Jewish man.
<br />
<br />
There is a difference between the laws of nazir for women and for slaves. For a woman, a husband can annul her vow, particularly of becoming a nazir (nazirah). The annulment is still active if they divorce or if the husband dies. However, with the slave, it is not so. While the master can force the slave to drink wine and go to a cemetery, this does not cancel the vow; when the slave goes free, he needs to complete his term of nazir.
<br />
<br />
Art: Slaves on the West Coast of Africa by Francois-Auguste Biard

### content/nazir/66.txt
<a id="src-00177"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nazir/66.txt`

Nazir 66 – Is it better to bless or to be blessed?
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;">
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEiF4YD58-FI9QWWAQZHtOAnY0f2aCi9gXxsNJI6x9YiLFGRDnYiiI2Thj7ppBFOESqVO8cK-2GHshKOhj-KATcWnc7XO0Bk86-XeiqEep5aw3DVh31kRvSiXEzUQ7KecIME8ACTeJGFy6pU/s1600/The+Soldier%2527s+Return+by+Girolamo+Induno.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="320" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEiF4YD58-FI9QWWAQZHtOAnY0f2aCi9gXxsNJI6x9YiLFGRDnYiiI2Thj7ppBFOESqVO8cK-2GHshKOhj-KATcWnc7XO0Bk86-XeiqEep5aw3DVh31kRvSiXEzUQ7KecIME8ACTeJGFy6pU/s320/The+Soldier%2527s+Return+by+Girolamo+Induno.jpg" width="244" /></a></div>
About Samuel the prophet, it says that a <a href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt08a01.htm">razor ("morah") will not pass over his hair</a>. This means that he was a nazir, just like Simeon, about whom the same is said. This is the opinion of Rabbi Nehorai. 
<br />
<br />
However, Rabbi Yose translates the word “morah” as fear and says that fear of man would never be upon Samuel. To this, Rabbi Nehorai replies that Samuel was afraid once when he said, “<a href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt08b16.htm">What if the king hears and kills me?</a>” The answer of Rabbi Nehorai is not given here.
<br />
<br />
The same Sages agree on something else, though. Rabbi Yose says that the one who responds with “Amen” (which signals his agreement to the blessing) is greater than the one who says the blessing. Rabbi Nehorai said, “By Heaven, so it is! Look at a battle: regular soldiers start, but champions seal the battle.” And yet, another Sage disagrees and says that one should rush to say the blessing first because he is then blessed from Heaven first – and afterward, the one who answers “Amen” receives the same reward (but not greater). 
<br />
<br />
And talking about battles, Sages, through their Torah study, increase peace in the world since their war of arguments substitutes for the physical conflict.
<br />
<br />
Art: The Soldier's Return by Girolamo Induno

### content/nazir/9.txt
<a id="src-00180"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nazir/9.txt`

Nazir 9 – Nazir who did not like figs
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEjes2td6N2Dkd0UoYHTa3JjGbft3JcgyRD4o3YdMtImugfjsNxV-F2RKi6e4ByBxRe_gCyaMZ2ni8DPoj7PretP_uRllapV3q601QdXRtI7JvZqMiUtvweo9q2kYMH0rssmdozjT0hsK_Fu/s1600/Melon+And+Bowl+Of+Figs+by+Gustave+Caillebotte.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="266" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEjes2td6N2Dkd0UoYHTa3JjGbft3JcgyRD4o3YdMtImugfjsNxV-F2RKi6e4ByBxRe_gCyaMZ2ni8DPoj7PretP_uRllapV3q601QdXRtI7JvZqMiUtvweo9q2kYMH0rssmdozjT0hsK_Fu/s320/Melon+And+Bowl+Of+Figs+by+Gustave+Caillebotte.jpg" width="320" /></a>If one says, "I am a nazir, so I cannot eat figs," – this is a strange statement. Being a nazir  means specifically abstaining from grapes, nothing else. However, Beit Shammai says that he does become a nazir nevertheless. How so? People usually do not make nonsensical statements. This one probably wanted to become a nazir but added that he meant figs. He could have made a mistake, thinking there was such a thing. Or, he really could have changed his mind and was preparing a loophole for himself. But the problem is that Beit Shammai does not accept the idea of changing one's mind regarding Temple-related things. So either way, he becomes a nazir.
<br />
<br />
What about Beit Hillel? They say that the man is not a nazir. He made a statement, that is true, but it was not a valid legal statement about becoming a nazir. So it did not take effect at all.
<br />
<br />
Art: Melon And Bowl Of Figs by Gustave Caillebotte

---

### content/nedarim/10.txt
<a id="src-00181"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nedarim/10.txt`

Nedarim 10 – Should one ever vow?
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEi3r-0rah-PqI0pda7ov8dMnXu6IEc5l6vVUAnZ5NYKPX_C3Kzxdpir6b1dk6H7Tk9YvuPsYIrfTaDUIHhZdNa9uBIzix88NtaEGWONClDuiP_rgTI0cULA8_kuJ0HwBHOwgbgrvb3Clgvy/s1600/A+Domestic+Affliction+by+Benjamin+West.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="253" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEi3r-0rah-PqI0pda7ov8dMnXu6IEc5l6vVUAnZ5NYKPX_C3Kzxdpir6b1dk6H7Tk9YvuPsYIrfTaDUIHhZdNa9uBIzix88NtaEGWONClDuiP_rgTI0cULA8_kuJ0HwBHOwgbgrvb3Clgvy/s320/A+Domestic+Affliction+by+Benjamin+West.jpg" width="320" /></a>In general, one should avoid making vows simply because he may transgress them. Better do something good without a promise, or at least promise right before the deed. For this reason, a Nazir is called “a transgressor” - because he abstains from wine, which is wrong. Now, if just for not drinking wine one is called a sinner, how much more so is one who afflicts himself with unnecessary deprivations and fasts.
<br />
<br />
If one does decide to make a vow, he has a choice of many words to use, “konam,” “konach,” “konas.” These were created by the Sages so that one should not use the word “korban” (sacrifice). But why not use the word “korban?” – Because he may say, “Korban to God.” But let him say that! – Because he may say just “To God,” thus using the name of God for no purpose. This all is the view of Resh Lakish.
<br />
<br />
However, Rabbi Yochanan says these terms translate the word “korban” into foreign languages. These words are adequate only if the user or those around him understand this language. 
<br />
<br />
Art:&nbsp;A Domestic Affliction by Benjamin West

---

### content/nedarim/11.txt
<a id="src-00182"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nedarim/11.txt`

Nedarim 11 – You are not kosher to me!
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEinAzSa6eierT_Nmk7G0g77sSo0FZOulPVLaUOqNMYvsfeHcDfqu_hPXYoy-wiIl9ICJ_TzI-3PIqBCELY8C0v3EWYkVtGK0fvSBpmgBZ10wrYdRZDtEdBgIQhYUPkQRObBcDnoS6tXNtrc/s1600/Girl+Eating+an+Apple+by+Godfried+Schalcken.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="320" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEinAzSa6eierT_Nmk7G0g77sSo0FZOulPVLaUOqNMYvsfeHcDfqu_hPXYoy-wiIl9ICJ_TzI-3PIqBCELY8C0v3EWYkVtGK0fvSBpmgBZ10wrYdRZDtEdBgIQhYUPkQRObBcDnoS6tXNtrc/s320/Girl+Eating+an+Apple+by+Godfried+Schalcken.jpg" width="249" /></a>One can use the language of “not kosher” to create a vow. For example, if one says, “It is not kosher what I eat of yours!” – he has, in fact, forbidden himself the food coming from his fellow, and it would be counted as if he violated the Torah if he did eat it.
<br />
<br />
He can also say, “It is not regular (chulin) food,” “not clean,” or “like the remainder of a sacrifice” (which can not be eaten).
<br />
<br />
However, watch for provisos and caveats: if one says, “It is not kosher what I do not eat of yours,” then he means that what he does NOT eat will be not kosher, but what he DOES eat will be kosher, and thus he created no vow and no prohibition.
<br />
<br />
Art:&nbsp;Girl Eating an Apple by Godfried Schalcken

---

### content/nedarim/12.txt
<a id="src-00183"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nedarim/12.txt`

Nedarim 12 – This to me is like that!
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEhAgmH6CdGLUJyzDmvq7y6XxEGbsnKAqLEM_K32UiX1NXgtB8R-3fy12mJGI8AbvIZD-eYpI9ItvaKZ6z9SAgDzQR6835PTlc8lvE0x0kUTRdHQXszLkC-116kgsSOI5I5GOyVn3Y-wy2UR/s1600/Entertaining+the+Guests+by+Pieter+Gerardus+Sjamaar.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="221" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEhAgmH6CdGLUJyzDmvq7y6XxEGbsnKAqLEM_K32UiX1NXgtB8R-3fy12mJGI8AbvIZD-eYpI9ItvaKZ6z9SAgDzQR6835PTlc8lvE0x0kUTRdHQXszLkC-116kgsSOI5I5GOyVn3Y-wy2UR/s320/Entertaining+the+Guests+by+Pieter+Gerardus+Sjamaar.jpg" width="320" /></a>One does not have to say the words "I vow." He can instead say, "This, to me, is like that." For example, if he says, "This bread to me is like the meat of a disqualified offering," – he is then forbidden to eat this bread.
<br />
<br />
Rami bar Chama asked a question: if one says, "This bread to me is like the meat of a peace offering," – what happens then? For most of the lifetime of the peace offering, it is prohibited to eat, so maybe his vow takes effect. But in the end, after its blood has been thrown on the Altar, the meat is eaten by people and guests, so maybe he meant this permitted state, and therefore the vow effects no prohibition?
<br />
<br />
The Talmud tries to compare this case to various others, where the answer is known but still does not come with a resolution.
<br />
<br />
Art: Entertaining the Guests by Pieter Gerardus Sjamaar

---

### content/nedarim/13.txt
<a id="src-00184"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nedarim/13.txt`

Nedarim 13 – Vow legalese
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;">
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEimIFGYzLTvr4XNWvIryebZjxfUlhZFEliEqWzs75TFDytwgTeaialiUIWaB-WkpoLavXxGNHnv-fCyL4Pu6lD1wU8vOAiX4NN4T5lpEBNAuiPq5Y8YynVide_T9VtuPoF_0OXbOjupHBI7/s1600/Funeral+of+Firstborn+by+Nikolai+Aleksandrovich+Yaroshenko.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="219" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEimIFGYzLTvr4XNWvIryebZjxfUlhZFEliEqWzs75TFDytwgTeaialiUIWaB-WkpoLavXxGNHnv-fCyL4Pu6lD1wU8vOAiX4NN4T5lpEBNAuiPq5Y8YynVide_T9VtuPoF_0OXbOjupHBI7/s320/Funeral+of+Firstborn+by+Nikolai+Aleksandrovich+Yaroshenko.jpg" width="320" /></a></div>
The Torah said, "One who vows a vow..." – why did it have to repeat the word? To teach us that if one connects his vow to some other prohibited thing, this thing must become prohibited through a declaration and not be already prohibited. For example, he can say, "This is like a sacrifice to me," because an animal is not born a sacrifice, but rather someone designates it so. If he says, by contrast, "This is like a firstborn (animal) to me," then no vow is created because a firstborn becomes consecrated as a sacrifice at birth by itself. Some make exceptions only for the firstborn, but the rule holds true otherwise.
<br />
<br />
Another essential rule to remember: vows are valid only for material objects. For example, one cannot say, "I vow not to talk to you," but he can say, "I vow for my mouth not to talk to you."
<br />
<br />
Art: Funeral of Firstborn by Nikolai Aleksandrovich Yaroshenko

---

### content/nedarim/14.txt
<a id="src-00185"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nedarim/14.txt`

### content/nedarim/2.txt
<a id="src-00191"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nedarim/2.txt`

Nedarim 2 – What is a vow?
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEgCfp4Ch49Ff9mo5SQfeIrxbwOl7q4b_IYJklk3POhklNdWsGEb3aG19g-1dgtcKV_-A1AXScb6FoXhWYp7gZlPpEb1oeBARlmI2h2ksmJ2HXZM5eEpjDLdk5-I6HwYJMHQ4bB0ItLOqh9_/s1600/Making+bread+by++Frank+Bramley.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="320" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEgCfp4Ch49Ff9mo5SQfeIrxbwOl7q4b_IYJklk3POhklNdWsGEb3aG19g-1dgtcKV_-A1AXScb6FoXhWYp7gZlPpEb1oeBARlmI2h2ksmJ2HXZM5eEpjDLdk5-I6HwYJMHQ4bB0ItLOqh9_/s320/Making+bread+by++Frank+Bramley.jpg" width="226" /></a>A man can vow against eating meat, for example, and then he will become liable for eating it as if it were non-kosher. He can use the words, “I am making a vow (neder),” but he can also say, “This is prohibited to me as  a sacrifice (“korban”). He can use a synonym, such as “konam” instead of a “korban.” Finally, he can say an incomplete vow, such as “I am separated from you” or “I am distanced from you,” and as a result, he will not be able to derive any benefit from his fellow who was the subject of his vow.
<br />
<br />
The same rule applies to oaths as well. But how is an oath different from a vow? – Vow is usually directed at an object (bread), but an oath talks about the person's action (eating this bread).
<br />
<br />
For the following ten pages, we will discuss incomplete vows, and now the Talmud discusses why the teacher chose to discuss incomplete vows, which were mentioned last, and not synonyms, which were mentioned first. Examples of word sequences are brought from all areas of law, such as adornments that women can wear on Shabbat, flour sacrifices in the Temple, and inheritance laws.
<br />
<br />
Art: Making bread by  Frank Bramley

---

### content/nedarim/23.txt

**Source file:** `content/nedarim/23.txt`

Nedarim 23 – Kol Nidre
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEjXl4BUzsb3egho6YHm0BLcCq8Wsujywnp1dcl-uDvhXuw3wk8SxDQaRngeUxO_gclLhrTWJqthm7kXd5qyNr5Oij-GTuKUlCYgB9aeA_sFAIpw112r6N2FBTatwfxBKTDF1fNc5Ud5U-8A/s1600/A+Seated+Young+Boy+Eating+Soup+by++Giacomo+Francesco+Cipper.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="320" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEjXl4BUzsb3egho6YHm0BLcCq8Wsujywnp1dcl-uDvhXuw3wk8SxDQaRngeUxO_gclLhrTWJqthm7kXd5qyNr5Oij-GTuKUlCYgB9aeA_sFAIpw112r6N2FBTatwfxBKTDF1fNc5Ud5U-8A/s320/A+Seated+Young+Boy+Eating+Soup+by++Giacomo+Francesco+Cipper.jpg" width="259" /></a>Rabbi Elazar ben Yakov spoke little, but whatever he said became accepted. He gave the following advice: one who wants to press his friend to eat with him should say, "Any vows that I will make will be void," and then tell his friend, "I vow that you will eat with me."
<br />
<br />
But this could not be: once his friend hears the annulment, he will pay no attention to his vow and surely won't eat with him!
<br />
<br />
Instead, Rabbi Elazar was hiding something. This is what he meant: if one uses a vow to press his friend to eat with him, then this is just a vow to create motivation, which is automatically invalid. On a different theme, if one wants his vows for the coming year to be ineffective, he should stand up at the beginning of the year, on Rosh Hashana, and say thus: "All vows (Kol Nidre) that I will vow throughout the year should be void!" Then, when he, later on makes a vow, it is void, provided that he remembers his initial stipulation.
<br />
<br />
But no, just the opposite; if he remembers, why is he making a vow now? – it is to overturn it! Instead, he should NOT remember, and his vow is null and void, according to his "Kol Nidre" declaration. 
<br />
<br />
Art:&nbsp;A Seated Young Boy Eating Soup by &nbsp;Giacomo Francesco Cipper

---

### content/nedarim/24.txt
<a id="src-00196"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nedarim/24.txt`

Nedarim 24 – Should one say “Kol Nidre?”
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEjZBGj8KQLKiWdVsauxwjdNaQl9XVRU4tSK_PxtSZzaHoToWEUDKEALHnnABxvg0a13WCvsuZ2gIm7UiAq1qfqo5G1MZEe-zY8UyGRrwvY4x6IH1kgeXMDnwA5-DZiK9kGAm0_10SctRwPi/s1600/Invitation+to+Dance+by+Peter+Baumgartner.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="264" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEjZBGj8KQLKiWdVsauxwjdNaQl9XVRU4tSK_PxtSZzaHoToWEUDKEALHnnABxvg0a13WCvsuZ2gIm7UiAq1qfqo5G1MZEe-zY8UyGRrwvY4x6IH1kgeXMDnwA5-DZiK9kGAm0_10SctRwPi/s320/Invitation+to+Dance+by+Peter+Baumgartner.jpg" width="320" /></a>On the <a href="http://mkerzner.blogspot.com/2015/06/nedarim-23-kol-nidre.html">previous page</a>,&nbsp;the teacher hid his advice while giving it. Although one can annul all his vows for the upcoming year by saying "Kol Nidre," the teacher left it unsaid, and only special people knew through careful reading what he meant. One Sage wanted to publicize the device, but Rava rebuked him: the teacher hid it, and you want to reveal it!?
<br />
<br />
Talmud also wants to know whether other Sages agreed with this teacher (Rabbi Elazar ben Yaakov). After multiple unsuccessful attempts, it finds clear proof that other Sages disagreed. Yet, the law follows Rabbi Elazar ben Yakov, and the annulment of all vows at the beginning of the year is permitted. 
<br />
<br />
We saw that when one presses his friend to accept an invitation and even vows to forbid something should the friend not accept, such a vow is not taken seriously – it is just a matter of speech, designed as motivation to accept.
<br />
<br />
There is another similar category: if one says that he just saw as many people on the road as those that went to Jerusalem, that is, 600,000, and if not, he is taking a vow to forbid himself something, this is just an exaggeration, and the vows does not need annulment by the Sage. A similar vow will be if he says, "I just saw a snake like a beam of a vine press!"
<br />
<br />
Art:&nbsp;Invitation to Dance by Peter Baumgartner

---

### content/nedarim/25.txt
<a id="src-00197"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nedarim/25.txt`

Nedarim 25 – Tricks when taking an oath
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEgAog_2NgLzeX_FGTO3-mlGHUxFJUdje-EXXZcVw2QISYSwQvO2lGU02h6UvqP1s90nwhtIr7Ao1-RsDDKuumNtvWbYy3KmJ7VLuDVSD-wHOLYbtcTIxXLhRrO4_DpZuuIBgA9PNLsoyHwc/s1600/An+Old+Woman+Weighing+Gold+Coins+by+Rembrandt+School.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="320" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEgAog_2NgLzeX_FGTO3-mlGHUxFJUdje-EXXZcVw2QISYSwQvO2lGU02h6UvqP1s90nwhtIr7Ao1-RsDDKuumNtvWbYy3KmJ7VLuDVSD-wHOLYbtcTIxXLhRrO4_DpZuuIBgA9PNLsoyHwc/s320/An+Old+Woman+Weighing+Gold+Coins+by+Rembrandt+School.jpg" width="270" /></a>Just as one can make a vow to motivate his friend to eat with him, and the vow won't be binding since he only meant it for friendly coercion, so one can take a similar oath, which will be no oath at all, and he won't violate a prohibition of swearing falsely.
<br />
<br />
For example, one can say, “I swear, and let all the fruit of this world be prohibited to me if I have not seen on the road as many as pilgrims going to Jerusalem!” 
<br />
<br />
But perhaps he saw ants and called them “pilgrims,” and is thus swearing truly!? – No, that cannot be because anyone who takes an oath does so on ordinary people's understanding of words, not on his terms.
<br />
<br />
But if so, why do the standard formulas mention “swear on our understanding, not on yours?” – That is to prevent incidents like the following. One person was going to swear that he returned the money to his fellow, who left it for safekeeping. He put the coins in a walking stick, asked the depositor to hold the stick while he himself was holding the Torah scroll, and swore that he returned the money and that it was in the depositor's hands. At which the depositor became so infuriated that the broke the stick, the money fell out, and it came out that man swore truly. 
<br />
<br />
But did not Moses himself say, “Keep in mind that you swear today to observe the Torah and do this on mine and God's understanding of this?” The Talmud then discusses all possible ways Moses could have put this into the oath to make the contract unbreakable but finds ways to misinterpret them and get out from under the obligation. In the end, the Talmud finds unbreakable verbiage, which is more complex, so it tells that Moses just used the shortest way. 
<br />
<br />
Art :An Old Woman Weighing Gold Coins by Rembrandt School

---

### content/nedarim/26.txt
<a id="src-00198"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nedarim/26.txt`

Nedarim 26 – Clear mistakes
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;">
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEieh4ch-3NwMNNB-zdUJWhG7xNPF_X7yAmreP4NSM7hpb-TmAuywZe-jnTTJuEBaocSEOhtBhs8H15I34xN8Ur5YW2x1-yHyVqC4mGsC8amJRkyBMWpIgXK-Ympnfxp-jBx7y5wkZh80dbN/s1600/Peasants+preparing+food+in+a+courtyard+by+North-Italian+School.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="248" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEieh4ch-3NwMNNB-zdUJWhG7xNPF_X7yAmreP4NSM7hpb-TmAuywZe-jnTTJuEBaocSEOhtBhs8H15I34xN8Ur5YW2x1-yHyVqC4mGsC8amJRkyBMWpIgXK-Ympnfxp-jBx7y5wkZh80dbN/s320/Peasants+preparing+food+in+a+courtyard+by+North-Italian+School.jpg" width="320" /></a></div>
One can make an obvious mistake in his vow; if so, the vow does not count, and he does not have to fulfill it. For example, “This food is prohibited to me by a vow if I ate anything today – or if I drank today” – and then he remembers that he did eat or drink.
<br />
<br />
Another example: “Let my wife be prohibited to me by a vow since she stole my wallet – or since she hit my son” – and it turns out that she did not do such a thing.  Yet another example: he sees people eating his dates and vows the dates to be prohibited to them, and then discovers that his father or his brother were with these people  – all such vows are void.
<br />
<br />
In the same way, mistaken oaths are void. For example, Rav Kahana and Rav Asi both took an oath that their teacher said something, and their oaths clearly contradicted. When it was found out that one of them was correct and supported by others, the oath was still not false because, as far as the other one could ascertain, he believed to be telling the truth. 
<br />
<br />
Art: Peasants preparing food in a courtyard by North-Italian School

### content/nedarim/3.txt
<a id="src-00202"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nedarim/3.txt`

Nedarim 3 – Do not delay
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEhLROcoeM7fJaNJ53w9ozqxqa17UzUiWETn8KUMt0DQR86F64T80I3r1vhGweSAWCYMy0PmSFicQ73lTzlOWWo-I5XBNdSTbdlitUD4xqUBnyC_Pt5LmZTA5YkchYgJqrhajtqq0UUEvVzN/s1600/A+seated+Peasant+eating+a+Meal+by+German+School.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="320" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEhLROcoeM7fJaNJ53w9ozqxqa17UzUiWETn8KUMt0DQR86F64T80I3r1vhGweSAWCYMy0PmSFicQ73lTzlOWWo-I5XBNdSTbdlitUD4xqUBnyC_Pt5LmZTA5YkchYgJqrhajtqq0UUEvVzN/s320/A+seated+Peasant+eating+a+Meal+by+German+School.jpg" width="237" /></a><a href="http://mkerzner.blogspot.com/2015/05/nedarim-2-what-is-vow.html">On the previous page</a>, we were left with the question of why the teacher sometimes chooses to explain the concepts out of order, while sometimes he does present them in the order of introduction. The answer is that the teacher loves complicated and non-obvious derivations and prefers to explain them first.
<br />
<br />
Just as one can vow not to eat, drink, or use an object, one can vow to become a Nazir so that he won't drink wine, cut his hair or go to a cemetery. The laws of vows and of Nazir are parallel: one should not violate his vow, nor should he delay it. 
<br />
<br />
However, how can one violate a "do not delay" to become a Nazir? All the time that he is not a Nazir, he is not, and there is no requirement to become one. And once he vows to be a Nazir, he is, and he is not delaying anything.
<br />
<br />
This situation can be found when he says, "I will be a Nazir before I die." Since he does not know when he will die, he should become a Nazir immediately because he might break his vow the next moment. He violates the "do not delay" prohibition when he does not become a Nazir. This is similar to the one who says to his wife, "Here is your divorce (Get) an instant before I die." Suppose, for example, she is married to a Kohen. In that case, she is immediately prohibited from eating Kohen's portion (terumah) since he may die the next moment, and it would come out that she ate terumah illegally. <br />
<br />
Art: A seated Peasant eating a Meal at German School

---

### content/nedarim/33.txt
<a id="src-00206"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nedarim/33.txt`

Nedarim 33 – He paid off your debt? – You don't owe him anything!
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEjUDkmXr9ox0DjPS5GwfsoKK-0Jys304b8CFb9gWLoA0NA8rZOrIEkrfNBVpUH4xGk_gwenjUZ6Xy2CwYjdfjcPa7jJO159VDlbpywNCGDHhbe3g1rCeVtMZ1zy1N35cF1MZXSdBlW2QIB8/s1600/Lost+by+Frederick+McCubbin.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="320" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEjUDkmXr9ox0DjPS5GwfsoKK-0Jys304b8CFb9gWLoA0NA8rZOrIEkrfNBVpUH4xGk_gwenjUZ6Xy2CwYjdfjcPa7jJO159VDlbpywNCGDHhbe3g1rCeVtMZ1zy1N35cF1MZXSdBlW2QIB8/s320/Lost+by+Frederick+McCubbin.jpg" width="201" /></a>If one person (A) makes a vow not to provide any benefit to his fellow B, he (A) is still allowed to pay off B's debts. How could it be? – By paying off B's debts,  A is simply "chasing a lion away from B's property." That is, he is precluding B's creditors from running after him at some future date, which is not a benefit.
<br />
<br />
But wait, it is a benefit, after all!? – This follows the opinion of Chanan, which we <a href="http://mkerzner.blogspot.com/2015/05/ketubot-108-one-who-puts-his-money-on.html">learned earlier</a>: if one goes overseas and leaves his wife without sustenance, and his fellow provides her with money on his own, the husband is not obligated to repay.
<br />
<br />
Are there any other opinions? – Yes, those who disagree with Chanan say it is a benefit. Or, if you want, I can explain that the rule about paying off debts is universally accepted. I can say that loan B does not have any fixed date for payment. Then the creditors will never come to B because the "future date" mentioned above does not exist! So A provided no benefit, and even though he vowed against B, he is still allowed to do this service.
<br />
<br />Another thing that A is allowed to do is pay the half-shekel obligation. Everyone had to pay the half-shekel yearly to provide money for daily sacrifices, but even if he did not pay in the end, he still gets the atonement; thus, paying it for one's fellow is not a benefit.
<br />
<br />
Finally, he is allowed to return the fellow's lost object because he is doing a mitzvah. If in his place, they pay for the trouble of bringing the lost object, he should donate this money to the Temple.
<br />
<br />
Art:&nbsp;Lost by Frederick McCubbin

---

### content/nedarim/35.txt
<a id="src-00208"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nedarim/35.txt`

Nedarim 35 – How strong are your vows?
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEgw8Tix6ZjwEad4ORnvjCvkJyK6XsAn_pppLy36TiGZcMNHZvmfKSYsonrIg8DVf2b8r_xaRikx4-qE88QsS9lHsBxKrvpbiay6tiWNxzNkfFyjx22iXTPIGDMtgRN7lolYOfKuTkZsHrCB/s1600/Studies+of+bread+by+Spanish+School.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="207" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEgw8Tix6ZjwEad4ORnvjCvkJyK6XsAn_pppLy36TiGZcMNHZvmfKSYsonrIg8DVf2b8r_xaRikx4-qE88QsS9lHsBxKrvpbiay6tiWNxzNkfFyjx22iXTPIGDMtgRN7lolYOfKuTkZsHrCB/s320/Studies+of+bread+by+Spanish+School.jpg" width="320" /></a>If one declares some object forbidden to himself by a vow, just like a sacrifice, is the prohibition really that strong? For example, if he uses this object after all, does he need to bring an additional sacrifice (<a href="http://mkerzner.blogspot.com/search/label/Me%27ilah">"Me'ila"</a>), just as he would have for misusing a sacrifice?
<br />
<br />
Perhaps we can prove this is so from the previous ruling. There,&nbsp;<a href="http://mkerzner.blogspot.com/2015/06/nedarim-33-he-paid-off-your-debt-you.html">one was allowed to return a lost object even to someone he vowed not to provide any benefit</a>. However, he could not take the reward for this but had to give it to the Temple treasury. Does this not prove the point?  – Actually, this is indeed strong proof. 
<br />
<br />
We find that Rabbi Meir and the Sages argued about this exact point. And since every Sage in the Talmud builds a system of consistent teachings without contradictions, we can rely on them to verify that both points of view are logical and can be valid. 
<br />
<br />
And what did Rabbi Meir and the Sages argue about? – If one prohibits himself from a loaf of bread by a vow and then eats it, he has to bring a Me'ila offering to atone - this is the opinion of Rabbi Meir. The Sages, however, say that since this loaf is not equally prohibited to all, the concept of Me'ila does not apply. And yet, since the previous rule proves that Rabbi Meir is right, the final law follows him.
<br />
<br />
Art:&nbsp;Studies of bread by Spanish School

---

### content/nedarim/36.txt
<a id="src-00209"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nedarim/36.txt`

Nedarim 36 – Sorry, I cannot teach you Torah
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEh28vttDmIAFp6OtYKnp8U5guU_XIkDfO2FkAqbKXIG6_DXJO6lL9T4EEE7E_HoAV4MrMtXSkqUvqlsGs2JcjreeAFBTzUcDUnuTs_JIlSz5JIGxu1EnbFbkZkVV4pp8SnEIRwHpqDHuaGN/s1600/A+Lady+With+Her+Daughter+And+Two+Sons+by+Dutch+School.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="205" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEh28vttDmIAFp6OtYKnp8U5guU_XIkDfO2FkAqbKXIG6_DXJO6lL9T4EEE7E_HoAV4MrMtXSkqUvqlsGs2JcjreeAFBTzUcDUnuTs_JIlSz5JIGxu1EnbFbkZkVV4pp8SnEIRwHpqDHuaGN/s320/A+Lady+With+Her+Daughter+And+Two+Sons+by+Dutch+School.jpg" width="320" /></a>One who vowed not to provide any benefit to his fellow is not allowed to teach him Torah; however, he is allowed to teach him the moral lessons and stories about the Torah (Midrash), as well as teach Torah to his sons and daughters.
<br />
<br />
Basically, why should Torah be taught for free? Moses said, "<a href="http://bible.ort.org/books/pentd2.asp?ACTION=displaypage&amp;BOOK=5&amp;CHAPTER=4#P5016">Look, I taught you the Torah, for free, just as God commanded me</a>," – and you should also teach for free. Then if so, why is teaching Torah considered a benefit, which one should not confer on his fellow if he vowed against him? – We mean teaching the written Torah, and one takes money for teaching the melody, not the words. Alternatively, the art of reading the written Torah is usually taught to small children, and one receives money for being their babysitter rather than a teacher. However, moral lessons are for adults, and one cannot take the reward.
<br />
<br />
The rule says one can teach the fellow's sons and daughters. This rule is the source for the view that Torah should be taught to women as well as men.<br />
<br />
Art:&nbsp;A Lady With Her Daughter And Two Sons by Dutch School

---

### content/nedarim/37.txt
<a id="src-00210"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nedarim/37.txt`

Nedarim 37 – Making money on the Torah?
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEhRYbmkadQ5eGQVt-2j-Wn_Wq0lNO-GI02B6VtbUNDQQUqGupxaVk1T3BJapKHLEcTLJhVE-Ht0rrPyU-sbGW9NloIdwDFWt81TzAIMp_Qq63xKQ3hOpAH05cO5W9_VKIoLGcZ3Pzy9rojk/s1600/Portrait+of+Artist%2527s+Children+by+Jan+Matejko.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="248" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEhRYbmkadQ5eGQVt-2j-Wn_Wq0lNO-GI02B6VtbUNDQQUqGupxaVk1T3BJapKHLEcTLJhVE-Ht0rrPyU-sbGW9NloIdwDFWt81TzAIMp_Qq63xKQ3hOpAH05cO5W9_VKIoLGcZ3Pzy9rojk/s320/Portrait+of+Artist%2527s+Children+by+Jan+Matejko.jpg" width="320" /></a>Earlier, we saw that <a href="http://mkerzner.blogspot.com/2015/06/nedarim-36-sorry-i-cannot-teach-you.html">one cannot take money to teach Torah</a>. We also noticed that when this teaching is for singing the notes of the Torah, as sung in the synagogue, or simply for watching the kids so that they don't run out during lessons, then getting paid for this is acceptable.
<br />
<br />
But which of these two reasons is the real one? Some say that the singing notes were actually given to Moses together with the rest of the Torah: when Ezra read Torah to the people, he made them "<a href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt35b08.htm">understand the reading</a>," – which is achieved with proper notes; thus, no money can be earned for teaching that.<br />
<br />
Others argue that "understanding the reading" refers to proper pronunciation since there are places in the Torah where the words should not be read the way they are written. However, the notes were introduced by the Sages of the court of Solomon later, and reward may be taken for teaching them.
<br />
<br />
According to the first point of view, one is not paid for singing but for watching the children. This sounds reasonable; what will the opposing side say? They will answer that boys and girls are equally taught and that little girls cause less trouble and don't run out of the room – so one is not paid for watching them, and we are back to paying for singing.
<br />
<br />
Art: Portrait of Artist's Children by Jan Matejko

---

### content/nedarim/38.txt
<a id="src-00211"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nedarim/38.txt`

Nedarim 38 - Only Moses had to observe the Torah
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;">
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEh_AkW7EEqmlT36dfQTIavKXa6fVVjvn2Eo_DbegN50OEdegJzoA3bpwO-PxW7vO_ICMnNXl0nagAEDFBQoJSniNmPFaz3eoCyvTC8X7lw-fAp_tK6lp5evoqQ5MoUyhJU5o9rh-kQDLcOC/s1600/Moses+with+the+Ten+Commandments+by+Philippe+de+Champaigne.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="320" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEh_AkW7EEqmlT36dfQTIavKXa6fVVjvn2Eo_DbegN50OEdegJzoA3bpwO-PxW7vO_ICMnNXl0nagAEDFBQoJSniNmPFaz3eoCyvTC8X7lw-fAp_tK6lp5evoqQ5MoUyhJU5o9rh-kQDLcOC/s320/Moses+with+the+Ten+Commandments+by+Philippe+de+Champaigne.jpg" width="254" /></a></div>
Rabbi Yose, the son of Rabbi Chanina, said, "The Torah was given only to Moses and his descendants" because it clearly states, "<a href="http://bible.ort.org/books/pentd2.asp?ACTION=displaypage&amp;BOOK=2&amp;CHAPTER=34">Carve for yourself</a>"&nbsp;and "<a href="http://bible.ort.org/books/pentd2.asp?ACTION=displaypage&amp;BOOK=2&amp;CHAPTER=34#P2521">Write for yourself</a>." However, Moses was friendly and gave it to all of Israel.<br />
<br />
Now, how could it be? Aren't all Israel obligated to fulfill the Torah? Moses himself said, "<a href="http://bible.ort.org/books/pentd2.asp?ACTION=displaypage&amp;BOOK=5&amp;CHAPTER=4#P5016">I was commanded by God at this time to teach you the laws and statutes</a>." – Sure, Moses was commanded, he alone, but he chose to teach to all.
<br />
<br />
But what about the song Moses wrote so that "<a href="http://bible.ort.org/books/pentd2.asp?ACTION=displaypage&amp;BOOK=5&amp;CHAPTER=31#P5742">it will be for God a testimony</a>?" What testimony is needed if they were not commanded to keep the Torah?
<br />
<br />
The Talmud concedes the point and explains that the Torah was given to all of Israel. However, only Moses and his descendants were initially given the power for logical analysis and deductions. Out of the goodness of his heart, he taught this to all.
<br />
<br />
Art: Moses with the Ten Commandments by Philippe de Champaigne

---

### content/nedarim/39.txt
<a id="src-00212"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nedarim/39.txt`

Nedarim 39 – Visiting the sick
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEiUtc46bgL0gRdRbLOTzpheec_M9L7Wq_p49qyMw1mvHTFTmIN8etbtFcSCHIcPHhKFghD6jExtHfgD4zuEJDqfXR4QvUz41JcERErD-nlovlwSP4BiCMT2eupV_mFC7mALrUX_RsQU9sU0/s1600/The+Sick+Room+by+Emma+Brownlow.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="257" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEiUtc46bgL0gRdRbLOTzpheec_M9L7Wq_p49qyMw1mvHTFTmIN8etbtFcSCHIcPHhKFghD6jExtHfgD4zuEJDqfXR4QvUz41JcERErD-nlovlwSP4BiCMT2eupV_mFC7mALrUX_RsQU9sU0/s320/The+Sick+Room+by+Emma+Brownlow.jpg" width="320" /></a>The hint to the mitzvah of visiting the sick comes from the Korah's rebellion. Moses said, “<a href="http://bible.ort.org/books/pentd2.asp?ACTION=displaypage&amp;BOOK=4&amp;CHAPTER=16#P4221">If these people die like all men and are visited</a>...” – and from here, we see that usually when people get sick before they die, it is expected that they should be visited. 
<br />
<br />
If one vows against a fellow, he may still visit him if the fellow gets sick, but he should stand and not sit. What are we talking about? If the visitor is prohibited from benefiting the sick one, then the visitor should  be allowed to sit: he is doing a mitzvah. And suppose the visitor is prohibited from receiving benefits from the visitor. In that case, no visits should be allowed since the visitor inevitably derives some benefit by entering the house, which is more protected than being in the street!?<br />
<br />
Really, possessions of the visitor are prohibited to the sick. So the visitor should be able to enter and even sit with the sick person. However, the visitor may sit more than necessary, going beyond the bounds of the mitzvah and providing the sick person with the extra benefit, for which one could even claim a reward. By doing this for free, the visitor would be violating the vow. Therefore, the Sages allowed him to stand – since he would leave on time – but not to sit.
<br />
<br />
Art: The Sick Room by Emma Brownlow

---

### content/nedarim/4.txt
<a id="src-00213"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nedarim/4.txt`

Nedarim 4 – Or perhaps, “Do not delay” does not apply?
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEjA86ipLm1pw-ArSJI2_T99fKQCN_-5doKQK9L6Sp5pq_NgnMkhRdLixE0h7FZo3MQXzXTtlMTIUq8bK1m717FXaEagRs8PYC_KwqrOI2UU8-pkqv8M8DdLyPW2uX556MM3egCPQwuyrzSL/s1600/Father+And+Daughter+In+A+Landscape+by+Pierre+Duval-Lecamus.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="257" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEjA86ipLm1pw-ArSJI2_T99fKQCN_-5doKQK9L6Sp5pq_NgnMkhRdLixE0h7FZo3MQXzXTtlMTIUq8bK1m717FXaEagRs8PYC_KwqrOI2UU8-pkqv8M8DdLyPW2uX556MM3egCPQwuyrzSL/s320/Father+And+Daughter+In+A+Landscape+by+Pierre+Duval-Lecamus.jpg" width="320" /></a>For the <a href="http://mkerzner.blogspot.com/2015/05/nedarim-3-do-not-delay.html">past two pages</a>, we discussed why the prohibition of "Do not delay" applies to Nazir. We even found a particular case when it would apply – when one says, "I will be a Nazir before I die."
<br />
<br />
However, is that even true? After all, the laws of Nazir are an unusual innovation not found in other parts of the Torah. What is unusual? – The fact that one can complete his vow of a Nazir by offering only one of the three required sacrifices; is not ideal, but acceptable. You might have thought that just as the laws of a Nazir are more lenient here, so too they are more forgiving in regards to the "Do not delay" requirement. For that, we have to rely on the rule of similarity: vows and Nazir are similar; as such, all laws of vows apply to the Nazir.
<br />
<br />
On the other hand, maybe applying the rule of similarity is an overkill? Perhaps a simple rule of "context" would work? "Context" means if we find a law in one case, it applies to all other similar areas. For example, the father can annul a vow of his daughter. Therefore, he should be able to annul other similar vows, such as if she wants to be a Nazir (technically, "Nazirah"). – No, that logic would not work: vows are stricter in that they have no limit, but a usual Nazir is for thirty days. So maybe that's why the father needs the power to annul a vow but not a Nazir vow. Thus, we are back to using the rule of similarity.
<br />
<br />
Art: Father And Daughter In A Landscape by Pierre Duval-Lecamus

---

### content/nedarim/40.txt
<a id="src-00214"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nedarim/40.txt`

Nedarim 40 – Curing the sick
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEhbzL8PCklzfXAp7CPFusREiYFgTDsHOD22oB3weSqMYfcdXt0Ht02EYAgbBj42diq3WEkGOkPiw-lP1_DT0E7vTXQ5n_uob9N6DE2d-fHTeCkM7R1V-IyRuYxaVMZ9ytIpXxSdgs75TCx1/s1600/The+Sick+Woman%25C2%25A0+by+Jan+Steen.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="285" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEhbzL8PCklzfXAp7CPFusREiYFgTDsHOD22oB3weSqMYfcdXt0Ht02EYAgbBj42diq3WEkGOkPiw-lP1_DT0E7vTXQ5n_uob9N6DE2d-fHTeCkM7R1V-IyRuYxaVMZ9ytIpXxSdgs75TCx1/s320/The+Sick+Woman%25C2%25A0+by+Jan+Steen.jpg" width="320" /></a>The mitzvah of visiting the sick has no limit. How so? Does it mean that the reward for it is limitless? – Why, all mitzvot are that way! Instead, even a person of great importance should visit people of more diminutive stature. Or it could mean that there is no limit to how many times one may see a sick person, provided that it does not tire the sick one.
<br />
<br />
Rabbi Akiva noticed that one of his students did not attend the classes. He visited the student and found him sick and in a dirty, dusty room. Rabbi Akiva cleaned the room and cared for the student – some say that because of this visit, the innkeeper started watching for the student more – and the student recovered. The student told Rabbi Akiva, "Teacher, you brought me back to life!" Rabbi Akiva then taught that one who does not visit the sick is as if he spills blood.<br />
<br />
When Rava would get sick, he first told no one – so that his luck would not turn to the worst through the people wishing him ill. But after three days, he would announce it, saying, "Whoever does not like me will rejoice, and then God will have mercy on me, just as He does for anyone over whom enemies rejoice, and my friends will pray for me."
<br />
<br />
Art: The Sick Woman&nbsp; by Jan Steen

---

### content/nedarim/41.txt
<a id="src-00215"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nedarim/41.txt`

Nedarim 41 – Moral lessons from Rav Ami
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEgi3vZf6xVOqEcGMUoZQ3-d2bVG7q3PdErdWrQehkWhyQJ_iZ8cRHkiWvmxFu4mhTJIlqoaDxk18mVKglgmWb-HisON1fFnvmaSROCZwtzvlSD6YUqsbkbmfntH26TazdX-DOGCA8QVguPo/s1600/My+Wife+and+I+by+Istvan+Desi-Huber.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="320" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEgi3vZf6xVOqEcGMUoZQ3-d2bVG7q3PdErdWrQehkWhyQJ_iZ8cRHkiWvmxFu4mhTJIlqoaDxk18mVKglgmWb-HisON1fFnvmaSROCZwtzvlSD6YUqsbkbmfntH26TazdX-DOGCA8QVguPo/s320/My+Wife+and+I+by+Istvan+Desi-Huber.jpg" width="242" /></a>Rabbi Ami was quoted earlier, so we continue with his teachings. When the Torah says, “<a href="http://bible.ort.org/books/pentd2.asp?ACTION=displaypage&amp;BOOK=5&amp;CHAPTER=28#P5653">Because you did not serve God when you had everything and lacked nothing, now you will serve your enemies in hunger and cold and nakedness... without anything</a>,” what does “without anything” mean? What does it add? – Without a wife, he has been without joy, blessing, goodness, Torah, protection, assistance, atonement, peace, and life, and he is considered only half-man.
<br />
<br />
Others say that it means without assistance, others - without understanding. One born with understanding has everything within him, but one born without it should work hard to achieve it. If he mastered it, what does he lack? And if he did not achieve understanding, what has he acquired?
<br />
<br />
Rav Yosef got sick and forgot his learning. His student Abaye often reminded him, "This is what you taught us, and you derived it from here."<br />
<br />
Rabbi Yehudah the Prince, who compiled and wrote down the Mishnah, initially had thirteen complete versions. He taught seven of these to Rabbi Chiya. Later he got sick and forgot the other six. However, a certain laundryman heard Rabbi Yehudah repeat them out loud, so now he taught them back. Rabbi Yehudah then said, "You made me!" - anyone who teaches a fellow Torah is as if he made him, just like Abraham and Sarah, who "<a href="http://bible.ort.org/books/pentd2.asp?ACTION=displaypage&amp;BOOK=1&amp;CHAPTER=12#P304">made souls in Charan</a>."<br />
<br />
Art:&nbsp;My Wife and I by Istvan Desi-Huber

### content/nedarim/43.txt

Nedarim 43 – Vagaries of vows
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEhtkL3cN9SqPYz1a-2D4_aiVxp3As0YRFPkiqk4Pi084EtH0uzijTosby3A9voo5yQ_jmFyjnZ7hHkkmOToB2KXUqQIBR8zb_sOHOcMG4yr5vQctTodUxj5u5mnd3XZzhxOc-iEWTn_DPsd/s1600/Feeding+Time+by+V.+Chevilliard.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="320" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEhtkL3cN9SqPYz1a-2D4_aiVxp3As0YRFPkiqk4Pi084EtH0uzijTosby3A9voo5yQ_jmFyjnZ7hHkkmOToB2KXUqQIBR8zb_sOHOcMG4yr5vQctTodUxj5u5mnd3XZzhxOc-iEWTn_DPsd/s320/Feeding+Time+by+V.+Chevilliard.jpg" width="252" /></a>If A is prohibited by a vow to receive any benefit from B, and then A does not have what to eat, what is B to do if he wants to feed A? B can go to a storekeeper whom he frequents and tell him, "A does not have what to eat, and I don't know what to do about it." The storekeeper can take the hint and feed A, and B can then repay him. This is allowed since B never explicitly promised to repay, so the storekeeper acts on his own accord.
<br />
<br />
If they are traveling together on the road and A does not have what to eat, B can give food as a present to other travelers, and they can feed A. If there is no traveler with them, B can put the food on a rock and say, "This food is ownerless, and anyone can take it," and A can then eat it.
<br />
<br />
Rabbi Yose does not allow B to feed A by putting food on a rock because it is evident that he is giving food as a gift. Some say that even passing through other travelers is not permitted by Rabbi Yose.
<br />
<br />
Art: Feeding Time by V. Chevilliard

---

### content/nedarim/44.txt
<a id="src-00218"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nedarim/44.txt`

Nedarim 44 – Because of the cheats
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEg5epmwumJxWqZQyqodbcRCenLvhaNtoBYlggB6vLdtSUYCiMCACz0UPLfPgBGg404Em3PZqsBDGTRlbs6mmzqyAYcxMWVu8tJod3i10kJnvHb_c9ge1kFAeMrhqhSslT2Ixd20fysRuVoL/s1600/Field+of+Flax+by+Edgar+Degas.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="237" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEg5epmwumJxWqZQyqodbcRCenLvhaNtoBYlggB6vLdtSUYCiMCACz0UPLfPgBGg404Em3PZqsBDGTRlbs6mmzqyAYcxMWVu8tJod3i10kJnvHb_c9ge1kFAeMrhqhSslT2Ixd20fysRuVoL/s320/Field+of+Flax+by+Edgar+Degas.jpg" width="320" /></a>People found the following loophole in order not to give tithes: they would declare their field ownerless – and ownerless fields do not need tithes – and then immediately acquire it back. In this way, they would not need to part with about 22% of the crop.
<br />
<br />
To counteract this, the Sages changed the nature of the ownership and decreed that anyone who declares his field ownerless is allowed to change his mind and get it back within the next three days. Now, he still owns the field and has to give tithes. So that people would take this decree seriously, the Sages had to allow one to get the field back even if someone had already taken possession of it. 
<br />
<br />
Why is this mentioned here? Because it seems to answer who is right in the previous question of feeding one who does not have what to eat – there Rabbi Yose <a href="http://mkerzner.blogspot.com/2015/07/nedarim-43-vagaries-of-vows.html">did not allow the circumvent a vow by declaring the food ownerless</a>. If at least after three days, one cannot retract, then such declarations are to be taken seriously, and the food in question is really ownerless and not subject to a vow, and the hungry person can eat it – despite what Rabbi Yose said.
<br />
<br />
However, the Talmud finds a way to explain this rule, even according to Rabbi Yose. Thus we do not have a definitive conclusion.
<br />
<br />
Art: Field of Flax by Edgar Degas

---

### content/nedarim/45.txt
<a id="src-00219"></a>

**Source file:** `content/nedarim/45.txt`

Nedarim 45 – When partners fight
<a href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEjY0Zu6dvhQIZoeic2H3LtgOMglidPTMKTMn_tPQYkI8CvwyD-TkgSpUyVukrh_IbMKhtQd8xxG_YABOv0tN_4BTnfGKbzjLfwk0llz_lF80jrxQMhUBbs1hNtURxcCkzSSm0pTCNsuYkWI/s1600/A+Dutch+Courtyard++by+Pieter+De+Hooch.jpg" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="280" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEjY0Zu6dvhQIZoeic2H3LtgOMglidPTMKTMn_tPQYkI8CvwyD-TkgSpUyVukrh_IbMKhtQd8xxG_YABOv0tN_4BTnfGKbzjLfwk0llz_lF80jrxQMhUBbs1hNtURxcCkzSSm0pTCNsuYkWI/s320/A+Dutch+Courtyard++by+Pieter+De+Hooch.jpg" width="320" /></a>If two people jointly owned a courtyard – a widespread situation – and then they had a falling out so that each prohibited himself, by a vow, to benefit from his fellow's property, they have a problem entering their own courtyard. Since the courtyard is not divided, each portion of it is jointly owned by both. Thus, when one treads on the ground, he is partially transgressing the property of his fellow, which is prohibited to him by a vow.
<br />
<br />
However, Rabbi Elazar ben Yakov says that each one enters his own property when he enters the courtyard. When people buy property in common, they divide the ownership of it for use by area and in time. Exactly which portion and at what time belongs to each is determined by the future event of him entering it. So, when he comes into the courtyard, we say that this is precisely what they had agreed to, and now his action clarifies their agreement. This idea of retroactive designation, that is, "I see what happens, and I say that in the past we had actually agreed to exactly this division," is called "breira" or "clarification." 
<br />
<br />
Thus, the argument between the Sages and Rabbi Elazar ben Yakov is whether or not we can apply "clarification" in this case. The Sages do not permit it, but Rabbi Yakov uses it because there is no other choice. Should the courtyard be larger so that they can divide? Even he would not allow them to use it until they split. 
<br />
<br />
Art: A Dutch Courtyard by Pieter De Hooch

---
